title="d:Q44547">edit on Wikidata</a>]</div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>Arecibo Telescope</b> was a 305&#160;m (1,000&#160;ft) <a href="/wiki/Spherical_reflector" class="mw-redirect" title="Spherical reflector">spherical reflector</a> <a href="/wiki/Radio_telescope" title="Radio telescope">radio telescope</a> built into a natural <a href="/wiki/Sinkhole" title="Sinkhole">sinkhole</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Arecibo_Observatory" title="Arecibo Observatory">Arecibo Observatory</a> located near <a href="/wiki/Arecibo,_Puerto_Rico" title="Arecibo, Puerto Rico">Arecibo, Puerto Rico</a>. A cable-mount steerable <a href="/wiki/Receiver_(radio)" class="mw-redirect" title="Receiver (radio)">receiver</a> and several <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">radar</a> transmitters for emitting signals were mounted 150&#160;m (492&#160;ft) above the <a href="/wiki/Dish_antenna" class="mw-redirect" title="Dish antenna">dish</a>. Completed in November 1963, the Arecibo Telescope was the world\'s largest single-aperture telescope for 53 years, until it was surpassed in July 2016 by the <a href="/wiki/Five-hundred-meter_Aperture_Spherical_Telescope" title="Five-hundred-meter Aperture Spherical Telescope">Five-hundred-meter Aperture Spherical Telescope</a> (FAST) in <a href="/wiki/Guizhou" title="Guizhou">Guizhou</a>, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>.\n</p><p>The Arecibo Telescope was primarily used for research in <a href="/wiki/Radio_astronomy" title="Radio astronomy">radio astronomy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_science" title="Atmospheric science">atmospheric science</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Radar_astronomy" title="Radar astronomy">radar astronomy</a>, as well as for programs that <a href="/wiki/Search_for_extraterrestrial_intelligence" title="Search for extraterrestrial intelligence">search for extraterrestrial intelligence</a> (SETI). Scientists wanting to use the observatory submitted proposals that were evaluated by independent scientific referees. <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> also used the telescope for <a href="/wiki/Near-Earth_object" title="Near-Earth object">near-Earth object detection programs</a>. The observatory, funded primarily by the <a href="/wiki/National_Science_Foundation" title="National Science Foundation">National Science Foundation</a> (NSF) with partial support from NASA, was managed by <a href="/wiki/Cornell_University" title="Cornell University">Cornell University</a> from its completion in 1963 until 2011, after which it was transferred to a partnership led by <a href="/wiki/SRI_International" title="SRI International">SRI International</a>. In 2018, a consortium led by the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Central_Florida" title="University of Central Florida">University of Central Florida</a> assumed operation of the facility.\n</p><p>The telescope\'s unique and futuristic design led to several appearances in film, gaming and television productions, such as for the climactic fight scene in the <a href="/wiki/List_of_James_Bond_films" title="List of James Bond films">James Bond</a> film <i><a href="/wiki/GoldenEye" title="GoldenEye">GoldenEye</a></i> (1995). It is one of the 116 pictures included in the <a href="/wiki/Voyager_Golden_Record" title="Voyager Golden Record">Voyager Golden Record</a>. It has been listed on the US <a href="/wiki/National_Register_of_Historic_Places" title="National Register of Historic Places">National Register of Historic Places</a> since 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nrhpreg_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nrhpreg-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The telescope was named an <a href="/wiki/List_of_IEEE_milestones" class="mw-redirect" title="List of IEEE milestones">IEEE Milestone</a> in 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>The NSF reduced its funding commitment to the observatory from 2006, leading academics to push for additional funding support to continue its programs. The telescope was damaged by <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Maria" title="Hurricane Maria">Hurricane Maria</a> in 2017 and was affected by earthquakes in 2019 and 2020. Two cable breaks, one in August 2020 and a second in November 2020, threatened the structural integrity of the support structure for the suspended platform and damaged the dish. Due to uncertainty over the remaining strength of the other cables supporting the suspended structure, and the risk of collapse owing to further failures making repairs dangerous, the NSF announced on November 19, 2020, that the telescope would be decommissioned and dismantled, with the <a href="/wiki/LIDAR" class="mw-redirect" title="LIDAR">LIDAR</a> facility remaining operational.<sup id="cite_ref-NSFrelease20-010_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NSFrelease20-010-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pmid33214727_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid33214727-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Before it could be decommissioned, several of the remaining support cables suffered a critical failure and the support structure, antenna, and dome assembly all fell into the dish at 7:55&#160;a.m. local time on December 1, 2020, destroying the telescope.<sup id="cite_ref-guardian_collapsed_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardian_collapsed-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ap_collapse_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ap_collapse-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The NSF decided in October 2022 that it would not rebuild the telescope or build a similar observatory at the site.\n</p>\n<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="General_information">General information</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arecibo_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: General information"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Comparison_FAST_Arecibo_Observatory_profiles.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Comparison_FAST_Arecibo_Observatory_profiles.svg/220px-Comparison_FAST_Arecibo_Observatory_profiles.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Comparison_FAST_Arecibo_Observatory_profiles.svg/330px-Comparison_FAST_Arecibo_Observatory_profiles.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Comparison_FAST_Arecibo_Observatory_profiles.svg/440px-Comparison_FAST_Arecibo_Observatory_profiles.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="384" /></a><figcaption>Comparison of the <a class="mw-selflink selflink">Arecibo</a> (top), <a href="/wiki/Five-hundred-meter_Aperture_Spherical_Telescope" title="Five-hundred-meter Aperture Spherical Telescope">FAST</a> (middle) and <a href="/wiki/RATAN-600" title="RATAN-600">RATAN-600</a> (bottom) radio telescopes at the same scale</figcaption></figure>\n<p>The telescope\'s main collecting dish had the shape of a <a href="/wiki/Spherical_cap" title="Spherical cap">spherical cap</a> 1,000 feet (305&#160;m) in diameter with an 869-foot (265&#160;m) <a href="/wiki/Radius_of_curvature" title="Radius of curvature">radius of curvature</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-arecibo-multifeed_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arecibo-multifeed-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and was constructed inside a <a href="/wiki/Karst" title="Karst">karst</a> <a href="/wiki/Sinkhole" title="Sinkhole">sinkhole</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-naic_about_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-naic_about-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The dish surface was made of 38,778 perforated aluminum panels, each about 3 by 7 feet (1 by 2&#160;m), supported by a mesh of steel cables.<sup id="cite_ref-arecibo-multifeed_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arecibo-multifeed-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The ground beneath supported shade-tolerant vegetation.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>The telescope had three <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">radar</a> transmitters, with <a href="/wiki/Effective_isotropic_radiated_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Effective isotropic radiated power">effective isotropic radiated powers</a> (EIRPs) of 22&#160;<a href="/wiki/Watt#Terawatt" title="Watt">TW</a> (continuous) at 2380&#160;MHz,<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 3.2&#160;<a href="/wiki/Watt#Terawatt" title="Watt">TW</a> (pulse peak) at 430&#160;MHz, and 200&#160;<a href="/wiki/Watt#Megawatt" title="Watt">MW</a> at 47&#160;MHz,<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> as well as an ionospheric modification facility operating at 5.1 and 8.175&#160;MHz.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>The dish remained stationary, while receivers and transmitters were moved to the proper focal point of the telescope to aim at the desired target.<sup id="cite_ref-Cohen2009_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen2009-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As a spherical mirror, the reflector\'s focus was along a line rather than at one point. As a result, complex line feeds were implemented to carry out observations, with each line feed covering a narrow <a href="/wiki/Frequency_range" class="mw-redirect" title="Frequency range">frequency band</a> measuring 10\xe2\x80\x9345&#160;MHz. A limited number of line feeds could be used at any one time, limiting the telescope\'s flexibility.<sup id="cite_ref-arecibo-multifeed_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arecibo-multifeed-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\nThe receiver was on an 820-tonne (900-short-ton) platform suspended 150&#160;m (492&#160;ft) above the dish by 18 main cables running from three <a href="/wiki/Reinforced_concrete" title="Reinforced concrete">reinforced concrete</a> towers (six cables per tower), one 111&#160;m (365&#160;ft) high and the other two 81&#160;m (265&#160;ft) high, placing their tops at the same elevation.<sup id="cite_ref-naic_about_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-naic_about-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Each main cable was a 8&#160;cm (3.1&#160;in) diameter bundle containing 160 wires, with the bundle painted over and dry air continuously blown through to prevent corrosion due to the humid tropic climate.<sup id="cite_ref-science_collapse_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-science_collapse-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The platform had a rotating, bow-shaped track 93&#160;m (305&#160;ft) long, called the <a href="/wiki/Azimuth" title="Azimuth">azimuth</a> arm, carrying the receiving antennas and secondary and tertiary reflectors. This allowed the telescope to observe any region of the sky in a forty-degree cone of visibility about the local <a href="/wiki/Zenith" title="Zenith">zenith</a> (between &#8722;1 and 38 degrees of <a href="/wiki/Declination" title="Declination">declination</a>). <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a>\'s location near the Northern <a href="/wiki/Tropic_of_Cancer" title="Tropic of Cancer">Tropic</a> allowed the Arecibo telescope to view the planets in the Solar System over the northern half of their orbit. The round trip light time to objects beyond <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a> is longer than the 2.6-hour time that the telescope could track a celestial position, preventing <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">radar</a> observations of more distant objects.<sup id="cite_ref-naic_about_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-naic_about-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto;overflow:hidden;width:auto;max-width:808px"><div class="thumbinner"><div class="noresize" style="overflow:auto"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Panorama_arecibo_telescope_from_observation_deck.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="The Arecibo Radio Telescope as viewed from the observation deck, October 2013"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Panorama_arecibo_telescope_from_observation_deck.jpg/800px-Panorama_arecibo_telescope_from_observation_deck.jpg" decoding="async" width="800" height="160" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Panorama_arecibo_telescope_from_observation_deck.jpg/1200px-Panorama_arecibo_telescope_from_observation_deck.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Panorama_arecibo_telescope_from_observation_deck.jpg/1600px-Panorama_arecibo_telescope_from_observation_deck.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5320" data-file-height="1066" /></a></span></div><div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Panorama_arecibo_telescope_from_observation_deck.jpg" title="File:Panorama arecibo telescope from observation deck.jpg"> </a></div>The Arecibo Radio Telescope as viewed from the observation deck, October 2013</div></div></div>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="History">History</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arecibo_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Design_and_construction">Design and construction</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arecibo_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Design and construction"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Arecibo_radio_telescope_SJU_06_2019_6144.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Arecibo_radio_telescope_SJU_06_2019_6144.jpg/290px-Arecibo_radio_telescope_SJU_06_2019_6144.jpg" decoding="async" width="290" height="435" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Arecibo_radio_telescope_SJU_06_2019_6144.jpg/435px-Arecibo_radio_telescope_SJU_06_2019_6144.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Arecibo_radio_telescope_SJU_06_2019_6144.jpg/580px-Arecibo_radio_telescope_SJU_06_2019_6144.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="3000" /></a><figcaption>The Arecibo radio telescope in 2019</figcaption></figure>\n<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Arecibo_Radiotelescopio_SJU_06_2019_7428.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Arecibo_Radiotelescopio_SJU_06_2019_7428.jpg/290px-Arecibo_Radiotelescopio_SJU_06_2019_7428.jpg" decoding="async" width="290" height="193" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Arecibo_Radiotelescopio_SJU_06_2019_7428.jpg/435px-Arecibo_Radiotelescopio_SJU_06_2019_7428.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Arecibo_Radiotelescopio_SJU_06_2019_7428.jpg/580px-Arecibo_Radiotelescopio_SJU_06_2019_7428.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2000" /></a><figcaption>A detailed view of the beam-steering mechanism. The triangular platform at the top was fixed, and the <a href="/wiki/Azimuth" title="Azimuth">azimuth</a> arm rotated beneath it. To the right was the Gregorian sub-reflector, and to the left was the remains of the 96-foot-long (29&#160;m) line feed tuned to 430 MHz (destroyed by Hurricane Maria). Also to the right was the catwalk and part of the rectangular <a href="/wiki/Waveguide" title="Waveguide">waveguide</a> that brought the 2.5 MW 430 MHz radar transmitter\'s signal up to the focal region.</figcaption></figure>\n<p>The origins of the observatory trace to late 1950s efforts to develop <a href="/wiki/Anti-ballistic_missile" title="Anti-ballistic missile">anti-ballistic missile</a> (ABM) defenses as part of the newly formed <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Defense" title="United States Department of Defense">United States Department of Defense</a> (DoD) <a href="/wiki/Advanced_Research_Projects_Agency" class="mw-redirect" title="Advanced Research Projects Agency">Advanced Research Projects Agency</a> (ARPA) ABM umbrella-effort, Project Defender. Even at this early stage it was clear that the use of <a href="/wiki/Chaff_(countermeasure)" title="Chaff (countermeasure)">radar decoys</a> would be a serious problem at the long ranges needed to successfully attack a warhead, ranges on the order of 1,600&#160;km (1,000&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-darpa_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-darpa-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-press_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-press-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>Among the many Defender projects were several studies based on the concept that a re-entering <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_warhead" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear warhead">nuclear warhead</a> would cause unique physical signatures while still in the upper atmosphere. It was known that hot, high-speed objects caused ionization of the atmosphere that reflects <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">radar</a> waves, and it appeared that a warhead\'s signature would be different enough from decoys that a detector could pick out the warhead directly, or alternately, provide added information that would allow operators to focus a conventional tracking radar on the single return from the warhead.<sup id="cite_ref-darpa_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-darpa-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-press_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-press-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>Although the concept appeared to offer a solution to the tracking problem, there was almost no information on either the physics of re-entry or a strong understanding of the normal composition of the upper layers of the <a href="/wiki/Ionosphere" title="Ionosphere">ionosphere</a>. ARPA began to address both simultaneously. To better understand the radar returns from a warhead, several radars were built on <a href="/wiki/Kwajalein_Atoll" title="Kwajalein Atoll">Kwajalein Atoll</a>, while Arecibo started with the dual purpose of understanding the ionosphere\'s F-layer while also producing a general-purpose scientific radio observatory.<sup id="cite_ref-darpa_18-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-darpa-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-press_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-press-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>On November 6, 1959, Cornell University entered into a contract with ARPA to carry out development studies for a large-scale ionospheric radar probe, exploring how this instrument could also be utilized in radio astronomy and other scientific areas.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The observatory was built between mid-1960 and November 1963. <a href="/wiki/William_E._Gordon" title="William E. Gordon">William E. Gordon</a> and George Peter of <a href="/wiki/Cornell_University" title="Cornell University">Cornell University</a> oversaw its design for study of the Earth\'s <a href="/wiki/Ionosphere" title="Ionosphere">ionosphere</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Gold_119_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gold_119-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fr\xc3\xa8_276_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fr\xc3\xa8_276-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mathews2013_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mathews2013-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cohen2009_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen2009-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> He was attracted to the <a href="/wiki/Sinkholes" class="mw-redirect" title="Sinkholes">sinkholes</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Karst" title="Karst">karst</a> regions of <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a> that offered perfect cavities for a very large dish.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Originally, a fixed parabolic reflector was envisioned, pointing in a fixed direction with a 150&#160;m (492&#160;ft) tower to hold equipment at the focus. This design would have limited its use in other research areas, such as <a href="/wiki/Radar_astronomy" title="Radar astronomy">radar astronomy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Radio_astronomy" title="Radio astronomy">radio astronomy</a> and atmospheric science, which require the ability to point at different positions in the sky and track those positions for an extended time as the Earth rotates.\n</p><p>Ward Low of the ARPA pointed out this flaw and put Gordon in touch with the <a href="/wiki/Air_Force_Cambridge_Research_Laboratories" class="mw-redirect" title="Air Force Cambridge Research Laboratories">Air Force Cambridge Research Laboratory</a> (AFCRL) in <a href="/wiki/Boston,_Massachusetts" class="mw-redirect" title="Boston, Massachusetts">Boston, Massachusetts</a>, where one group headed by Phil Blacksmith was working on spherical reflectors and another group was studying the propagation of <a href="/wiki/Radio_wave" title="Radio wave">radio waves</a> in and through the upper atmosphere. Cornell University proposed the project to ARPA in mid-1958 and a contract was signed between the AFCRL and the University in November 1959. Cornell University and Zachary Sears published a request for proposals (RFP) asking for a design to support a <a href="/wiki/Antenna_feed" title="Antenna feed">feed</a> moving along a spherical surface 133 metres (435&#160;ft) above the stationary reflector. The RFP suggested a tripod or a tower in the center to support the feed. On the day the project for the design and construction of the antenna was announced at Cornell University, Gordon had also envisioned a 133&#160;m (435&#160;ft) tower centered in the 305&#160;m (1,000&#160;ft) reflector to support the feed.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mathews2013_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mathews2013-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/George_Doundoulakis" title="George Doundoulakis">George Doundoulakis</a>, who directed research at the <a href="/wiki/General_Bronze_Corporation" title="General Bronze Corporation">General Bronze Corporation</a> in <a href="/wiki/Garden_City,_New_York" title="Garden City, New York">Garden City, New York</a>, along with Zachary Sears, who directed Internal Design at Digital B &amp; E Corporation, New York, received the RFP from <a href="/wiki/Cornell_University" title="Cornell University">Cornell University</a> for the antenna design and studied the idea of suspending the feed with his brother, <a href="/wiki/Helias_Doundoulakis" title="Helias Doundoulakis">Helias Doundoulakis</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Civil_engineer" title="Civil engineer">civil engineer</a>. George Doundoulakis identified the problem that a tower or tripod would have presented around the center, (the most important area of the reflector), and devised a better design by suspending the feed.<sup id="cite_ref-Fr\xc3\xa8_276_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fr\xc3\xa8_276-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gold_119_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gold_119-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> He presented his proposal to <a href="/wiki/Cornell_University" title="Cornell University">Cornell University</a> for a doughnut or <a href="/wiki/Torus" title="Torus">torus</a>-type <a href="/wiki/Truss" title="Truss">truss</a> suspended by four cables from four towers above the reflector, having along its edge a rail track for the <a href="/wiki/Azimuth" title="Azimuth">azimuthal</a> truss positioning. This second truss, in the form of an arc, or <a href="/wiki/Arch" title="Arch">arch</a>, was to be suspended below, which would rotate on the rails through 360 degrees. The arc also had rails on which the unit supporting the feed would move for the feed\'s elevational positioning. A <a href="/wiki/Counterweight" title="Counterweight">counterweight</a> would move symmetrically opposite to the feed for stability and, if a hurricane struck, the whole feed could be raised and lowered. Helias Doundoulakis designed the <a href="/wiki/Wire_rope" title="Wire rope">cable</a> suspension system which was finally adopted. The final configuration was substantially the same as in the original drawings by George and Helias Doundoulakis, although with three towers, instead of the four drawn in the patent, which was granted to <a href="/wiki/Helias_Doundoulakis" title="Helias Doundoulakis">Helias Doundoulakis</a> by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Patent_and_Trademark_Office" title="United States Patent and Trademark Office">U.S. Patent office</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cooke1976_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cooke1976-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>The suspended structure was designed by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Christian_Kavanagh" title="Thomas Christian Kavanagh">Dr. Thomas C. Kavanagh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fred_Severud" title="Fred Severud">Fred Severud</a>, and Dr. Hans Bandel, who were selected after the 1959 RFP issued by Cornell University. A proposal by the <a href="/wiki/General_Bronze_Corporation" title="General Bronze Corporation">General Bronze Corporation</a> was not selected as it did not meet specifications, according to an editorial response by Donald Cooke (Cornell\'s spokesperson) to <a href="/wiki/Helias_Doundoulakis" title="Helias Doundoulakis">Helias Doundoulakis</a> in a newsletter of the Institute of Electrical and Electronics Engineers (<i>IEEE</i>). Cooke stated that Doundoulakis used an incorrect feed/paraxial surface measurement. However, the measurement Cooke used was from Doundoulakis\xe2\x80\x99 patent issued in 1966, and not from the 1959 RFP meetings which predated the patent by seven years.<sup id="cite_ref-Cooke1976_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cooke1976-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Furthermore, proposal measurements presented by <a href="/wiki/George_Doundoulakis" title="George Doundoulakis">George Doundoulakis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Helias_Doundoulakis" title="Helias Doundoulakis">Helias Doundoulakis</a> at the RFP meeting on December 10, 1959, were not referenced in Cooke\'s editorial response.<sup id="cite_ref-Cooke1976_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cooke1976-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The originators of this proposal subsequently filed a dispute, originally for $1.2 million but was settled for $10,000 because "the defense in a court trial would cost far more than the $10,000 for which the case was settled," and accordingly, on April 11, 1975, Doundoulakis v. U.S. (Case 412-72) had been ruled in plaintiff\'s favor by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Court_of_Federal_Claims" title="United States Court of Federal Claims">United States Court of Federal Claims</a>, that \xe2\x80\x9c(a) a judgment has been entered in favor of the plaintiffs (<a href="/wiki/Helias_Doundoulakis" title="Helias Doundoulakis">Helias Doundoulakis</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_J._Casey" title="William J. Casey">William J. Casey</a>, and Constantine Michalos) against the United States and (b) in consideration of the sum of $10,000 to be paid by the United States Government to the plaintiff, the plaintiffs grants to the United States Government an irrevocable, fully-paid, non-exclusive license under the aforesaid U.S. Patent No. 3, 273, 156 to Cornell University.\xe2\x80\x9d<sup id="cite_ref-Cooke1976_30-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cooke1976-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>The idea of a spherical reflecting mirror with a steerable secondary has since been used in optical telescopes, in particular, the <a href="/wiki/Hobby%E2%80%93Eberly_Telescope" title="Hobby\xe2\x80\x93Eberly Telescope">Hobby\xe2\x80\x93Eberly Telescope</a><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>Construction began in mid-1960, with the telescope operational about three years later. The telescope\'s and the supporting observatory\'s official opening as the Arecibo Ionospheric Observatory (AIO) was held on November 1, 1963.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-congrecord_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-congrecord-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Upgrades">Upgrades</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arecibo_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Upgrades"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<p>Since its construction, the telescope was upgraded several times, following the facility\'s oversight from the DoD to the <a href="/wiki/National_Science_Foundation" title="National Science Foundation">National Science Foundation</a> on October 1, 1969, and subsequent renaming of the AIO to the National Astronomy and Ionosphere Center (NAIC) in September 1971.<sup id="cite_ref-science_collapse_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-science_collapse-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-congrecord_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-congrecord-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Initially, when the maximum expected operating frequency was about 500&#160;MHz, the surface consisted of half-inch galvanized wire mesh laid directly on the support cables. In 1973, a high-precision surface consisting of 38,000 individually adjustable aluminum panels replaced the old wire mesh,<sup id="cite_ref-SP4218_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SP4218-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and the highest usable frequency rose to about 5000&#160;MHz. A <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_telescope" title="Gregorian telescope">Gregorian reflector system</a> was installed in 1997, incorporating secondary and tertiary reflectors to focus radio waves at one point. This allowed installing a suite of receivers, covering the full 1\xe2\x80\x9310&#160;GHz range, that could be easily moved to the <a href="/wiki/Focus_(optics)" title="Focus (optics)">focal point</a>, giving Arecibo more flexibility. The additional instrumentation added 270-tonne (300-short-ton) to the platform, so six additional support cables were added, two for each tower.<sup id="cite_ref-science_collapse_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-science_collapse-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A metal mesh screen was also installed around the perimeter to block the ground\'s thermal radiation from reaching the feed antennas. As part of this upgrade the power of the 2380&#160;MHz transmitter was doubled to 1 MW by adding a second Klystron tube and improving the design.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Finally, in 2013 with a grant of <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$2.5 million</span>, work for adding the ionospheric modification HF facility began which was completed in 2015. The HF facility consisted on the sender side of six foldable 100&#160;kW crossed dipoles inside the main dish and a hanging 100m wide subreflector mesh between the dish and platform.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto;overflow:hidden;width:auto;max-width:1208px"><div class="thumbinner"><div class="noresize" style="overflow:auto"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Arecibo_Radiotelescopio_Panamorama_SJU_06_2019_7446.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="Panoramic view of the Arecibo radio telescope primary dish in June 2019. The HF transmitting antennas can be seen at the base of the dish."><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Arecibo_Radiotelescopio_Panamorama_SJU_06_2019_7446.jpg/1200px-Arecibo_Radiotelescopio_Panamorama_SJU_06_2019_7446.jpg" decoding="async" width="1200" height="369" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Arecibo_Radiotelescopio_Panamorama_SJU_06_2019_7446.jpg/1800px-Arecibo_Radiotelescopio_Panamorama_SJU_06_2019_7446.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Arecibo_Radiotelescopio_Panamorama_SJU_06_2019_7446.jpg/2400px-Arecibo_Radiotelescopio_Panamorama_SJU_06_2019_7446.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6038" data-file-height="1856" /></a></span></div><div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Arecibo_Radiotelescopio_Panamorama_SJU_06_2019_7446.jpg" title="File:Arecibo Radiotelescopio Panamorama SJU 06 2019 7446.jpg"> </a></div>Panoramic view of the Arecibo radio telescope primary dish in June 2019. The HF transmitting antennas can be seen at the base of the dish.</div></div></div>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Funding_reductions">Funding reductions</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arecibo_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Funding reductions"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<p>The Astronomical Sciences and Atmospheric Sciences divisions of the NSF had financially supported Arecibo since its completion in the 1970s, with incremental support by NASA, for operating the planetary radar.<sup id="cite_ref-SP4218_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SP4218-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 2001 NASA announced a rampdown and elimination of its support of the planetary radar by 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2002, after several years of discussion, the US Congress passed a bill to double the NSF budget, and instructed the NSF to begin new projects.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As a result, the NSF began committing to major projects. However, the funding increase never arrived and the NSF was left with the new commitments. In 2005 the Astronomical Sciences division commissioned a "Senior Review" of its facilities to deal with its increasingly constrained budget. The Senior Review report released in November 2006 "regretfully" recommended substantially decreased astronomy funding for the Arecibo Observatory, beginning with a cut to <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$10.5 million</span> in 2007 and continuing to decrease to <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$4.0 million</span> in 2011. The report further stated that if other sources of funding could not be found, closure of the Observatory was recommended.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>Academics and researchers responded by organizing to protect and advocate for the observatory. They established the Arecibo Science Advocacy Partnership (ASAP) in 2008, to advance the scientific excellence of Arecibo Observatory research and to publicize its accomplishments in astronomy, aeronomy and planetary radar as to seek additional funding support for the observatory.<sup id="cite_ref-areciboscience.org_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-areciboscience.org-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> An additional <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$3 million</span> in bonds were issued by the government of Puerto Rico to fund the Observatory, which were used to modernize power generation and improve other aging infrastructure.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Academics, media and influential politicians pressured the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">United States Congress</a> on the importance of the work of the observatory.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> led to additional <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$3.1 million</span> in funding to support Arecibo in the <a href="/wiki/American_Recovery_and_Reinvestment_Act_of_2009" title="American Recovery and Reinvestment Act of 2009">American Recovery and Reinvestment Act of 2009</a>. This was used for basic maintenance and for a second, much smaller, antenna to be used for <a href="/wiki/Very_long_baseline_interferometry" class="mw-redirect" title="Very long baseline interferometry">very long baseline interferometry</a>, new <a href="/wiki/Klystron" title="Klystron">Klystron</a> amplifiers for the <a href="/wiki/Radar_astronomy" title="Radar astronomy">planetary radar</a> system and student training.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>Arecibo\'s budget from NSF continued to wane in the following years.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2011budget_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2011budget-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Starting in FY2010, NASA restored its historical support by contributing $2.0&#160;million per year for <a href="/wiki/Planetary_science" title="Planetary science">planetary science</a>, particularly the study of <a href="/wiki/Near-Earth_object" title="Near-Earth object">near-Earth objects</a>, at Arecibo. NASA implemented this funding through its Near Earth Object Observations program.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> NASA increased its support to $3.5&#160;million per year in 2012.\n</p><p>In 2011, NSF removed <a href="/wiki/Cornell_University" title="Cornell University">Cornell University</a>, which had managed the <a href="/wiki/Arecibo_Observatory" title="Arecibo Observatory">National Astronomy and Ionosphere Center (NAIC)</a> since the 1970s, as the operator and transferred these responsibilities to <a href="/wiki/SRI_International" title="SRI International">SRI International</a>, along with two other managing partners, <a href="/wiki/Universities_Space_Research_Association" title="Universities Space Research Association">Universities Space Research Association</a> and <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_University_(Puerto_Rico)" class="mw-redirect" title="Metropolitan University (Puerto Rico)">Universidad Metropolitana de Puerto Rico</a>, with a number of other collaborators.<sup id="cite_ref-sri_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sri-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> NSF also decertified NAIC as a <a href="/wiki/Federally_Funded_Research_and_Development_Center" class="mw-redirect" title="Federally Funded Research and Development Center">Federally Funded Research and Development Center (FFRDC)</a>, which the NSF said would give NAIC greater freedom to establish broader scientific partnerships and pursue funding opportunities for activities beyond the scope of those supported by NSF,<sup id="cite_ref-2011budget_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2011budget-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> but which would also remove the <a href="/wiki/FFRDC" class="mw-redirect" title="FFRDC">FFRDC</a>\'s promise of stability intended to retain the very best technical staff.\n</p><p>While the Observatory continued to operate under the reduced NSF budget and NASA funds, NSF signaled in 2015 and 2016 that it was looking towards potential decommissioning of the Observatory by initiating <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_statement" title="Environmental impact statement">environmental impact statements</a> on the effect of disassembling the unit.<sup id="cite_ref-natgeo_2016_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-natgeo_2016-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The NSF continued to indicate it would like to reduce funding to the Observatory in the short term.<sup id="cite_ref-DCL2015_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DCL2015-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DCL2016_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DCL2016-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As in 2008, academics expressed their concern over the loss of scientific discoveries that could occur should the Observatory be shut down.<sup id="cite_ref-natgeo_2016_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-natgeo_2016-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="2020_damage,_decommissioning_plans,_and_collapse"><span id="2020_damage.2C_decommissioning_plans.2C_and_collapse"></span>2020 damage, decommissioning plans, and collapse</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arecibo_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 2020 damage, decommissioning plans, and collapse"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Arecibo_observatory_damage_map.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Arecibo_observatory_damage_map.svg/220px-Arecibo_observatory_damage_map.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="275" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Arecibo_observatory_damage_map.svg/330px-Arecibo_observatory_damage_map.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Arecibo_observatory_damage_map.svg/440px-Arecibo_observatory_damage_map.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="640" /></a><figcaption>Map of Arecibo Observatory after November 2020 cable damage<sup id="cite_ref-pmid33214727_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid33214727-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></figcaption></figure>\n<p>Several hurricanes and storms over the 2010s had raised the concerns of structural engineers over the stability of the observatory.<sup id="cite_ref-verge_decommission_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-verge_decommission-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> On September 21, 2017, high winds associated with <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Maria" title="Hurricane Maria">Hurricane Maria</a> caused the 430&#160;MHz line feed to break and fall onto the primary dish, damaging roughly 30 of the 38,000 aluminum panels. Most Arecibo observations did not use the line feed but instead relied on the feeds and receivers located in the dome. Overall, the damage inflicted by Maria was minimal,<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-auto_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-auto1_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> but it further clouded the observatory\'s future. Restoring all the previous capabilities required more than the observatory\'s already-threatened operating budget, and users feared the decision would be made to decommission it instead.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>A consortium consisting of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Central_Florida" title="University of Central Florida">University of Central Florida</a> (UCF), Yang Enterprises and <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_University_(Puerto_Rico)" class="mw-redirect" title="Metropolitan University (Puerto Rico)">UMET</a>, came forward to supply funding in February 2018 to allow the NSF to reduce its contribution towards Arecibo\'s operating costs from $8&#160;million to $2&#160;million from the fiscal year 2022\xe2\x80\x932023, thus securing the observatory\'s future.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> With this, the UCF consortium were named the new operators of the observatory in 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ucf_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ucf-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>On August 10, 2020, an auxiliary platform support cable separated from Tower 4, causing damage to the telescope, including a 100&#160;ft (30&#160;m) gash in the reflector dish.<sup id="cite_ref-UCFtodayBroken_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UCFtodayBroken-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Damage included six to eight panels in the Gregorian dome, and to the platform used to access the dome. No one was reported to have been hurt by the partial collapse. The facility was closed as damage assessments were made.<sup id="cite_ref-bi_2020_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bi_2020-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>The facility had recently reopened following the passing of <a href="/wiki/Tropical_Storm_Isaias" class="mw-redirect" title="Tropical Storm Isaias">Tropical Storm Isaias</a>. It was unclear if the cable failure was caused by Isaias. Former Arecibo Observatory director Robert Kerr stated that prior to the 1997 installation of the Gregorian dome, the main support cables and support towers had been engineered with a safety factor of two, as to be able to sustain twice the weight of the platform. When the dome was added in 1997, the auxiliary cables were intended to retain the safety factor of two once all design factors were considered, but Kerr believed that that was never the case as evenly distributing the loads following that install would be difficult to do.<sup id="cite_ref-science_collapse_16-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-science_collapse-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Kerr also stated that there had been periods of neglect at the Observatory, during which the fans that were used to blow dry air along the wire bundles were not operating. The earlier storms would have brought seawater to the cables which could accelerate the rate of corrosion as well, according to Kerr.<sup id="cite_ref-science_collapse_16-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-science_collapse-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Engineering firms hired by UCF inspected the socket area where the cable had failed, and found a similar problem that had been observed in the 1980s during a routine cable replacement, in which the use of molten zinc to affix the cable to the socket mount at the tower was not complete, allowing moisture to get into the wire bundle and cause corrosion and leading to the cable slipping from its socket.<sup id="cite_ref-science_collapse_16-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-science_collapse-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The firms had developed models of the telescope that showed that the safety factor for Tower 4 had dropped to 1.67, believing that the structure was still safe while repairs could be effected, even if another cable collapsed.<sup id="cite_ref-science_collapse_16-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-science_collapse-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Plans were made to replace all six auxiliary cables since their socket welds were all considered suspect at a cost of <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$10.5 million</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-science_collapse_16-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-science_collapse-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>Before repairs could be started, on November 7, 2020, one of the two main support cables from Tower 4 snapped, shattering part of the dish itself as it fell.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The UCF engineering staff, which had been monitoring the cables with support from the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Army_Corps_of_Engineers" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Army Corps of Engineers">U.S. Army Corps of Engineers</a>, and the engineering firms they had hired previously evaluated the remaining cables from Tower 4. One engineering firm proposed stabilization efforts,<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> while another suggested that they try to sever parts of the instrument platform such as the Gregorian dome to reduce the load.<sup id="cite_ref-science_collapse_16-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-science_collapse-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The third firm made the determination that there was no way to safely repair the damage at this point, as the remaining cables could be suspect, and furthermore that a controlled decommissioning of the telescope was the only effective means to avoid catastrophic failure which would threaten the other buildings on campus.<sup id="cite_ref-drake_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-drake-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The NSF took this advice and made the announcement on November 19, 2020 that they would decommission Arecibo over the following few weeks after determining the safest route to do so with a safety exclusion zone immediately put in place.<sup id="cite_ref-NSFrelease20-010_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NSFrelease20-010-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> NSF\'s Sean Jones stated, "This decision is not an easy one for NSF to make, but safety of people is our number one priority." The <a href="/wiki/Lidar" title="Lidar">lidar</a> facility will remain operational.<sup id="cite_ref-verge_decommission_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-verge_decommission-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NSFrelease20-010_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NSFrelease20-010-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>While waiting for NSF to make the decommissioning plans, steps had been taken to try to reduce the load that each of the towers were carrying, including reducing the strain on the backstay support cables for the individual towers. Other plans, such as having helicopters hoisting part of the load while hovering above the telescope, were proposed but deemed too risky.<sup id="cite_ref-engadget_nsf_footage_collapse_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-engadget_nsf_footage_collapse-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Engineers from UCF had been monitoring the telescope and observed that wires in the backstay cables for the support towers had been breaking at a rate of one or two a day, and estimated that the telescope would soon collapse.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In the weekend prior to December 1, 2020, wire strands in the receiver\'s supporting cables had also been snapping apart at a rapid rate, according to \xc3\x81ngel V\xc3\xa1zquez, the director of operations. This culminated in the collapse of the receiver platform at around 6:55&#160;a.m. AST (10:55 UTC) on December 1, 2020, as the second main cable from Tower 4 failed with the other two remaining support cables failing moments later. The collapse of the receiver structure and cables onto the dish caused extensive additional damage.<sup id="cite_ref-guardian_collapsed_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardian_collapsed-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ap_collapse_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ap_collapse-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-upi_collapse_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-upi_collapse-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As the receiver fell, it also sheared the tips of the towers which the support cables ran through. Once the main cables from Tower 4 released, the backstay cables, which normally balanced the horizontal component of force from the main cables, pulled the tower outwards and broke off the top. The other two towers, once the force of supporting the platform was released, also had their tips sheared off due to the backstay cable tension.<sup id="cite_ref-engadget_nsf_footage_collapse_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-engadget_nsf_footage_collapse-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The top of Tower 12 caused some structural damage to other buildings on the observatory as it fell. No injuries from the collapse were reported.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-engadget_nsf_footage_collapse_72-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-engadget_nsf_footage_collapse-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-collapsed_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-collapsed-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1237032888/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti
