data-transcodekey="360p.webm" data-width="640" data-height="360" /></video></span></span></div><div class="thumbcaption">Synchronized views of the Arecibo Telescope collapse.</div></div></div></div></div>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Post-collapse">Post-collapse</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arecibo_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Post-collapse"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Arecibo_Telescope,_December_2021.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Arecibo_Telescope%2C_December_2021.jpg/220px-Arecibo_Telescope%2C_December_2021.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="148" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Arecibo_Telescope%2C_December_2021.jpg/330px-Arecibo_Telescope%2C_December_2021.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Arecibo_Telescope%2C_December_2021.jpg/440px-Arecibo_Telescope%2C_December_2021.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1073" /></a><figcaption>The Arecibo Telescope during demolition process, December 2021</figcaption></figure>\n<p>In the weeks following Arecibo\'s collapse, the administration of the <a href="/wiki/Five-hundred-metre_Aperture_Spherical_Telescope" class="mw-redirect" title="Five-hundred-metre Aperture Spherical Telescope">Five-hundred-metre Aperture Spherical Telescope</a> (FAST) in China, which had drawn some design principles from Arecibo, stated that they would start taking applications for international researchers to use the telescope starting in 2021.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>In late December 2020, <a href="/wiki/Wanda_V%C3%A1zquez_Garced" title="Wanda V\xc3\xa1zquez Garced">Wanda V\xc3\xa1zquez Garced</a>, then governor of Puerto Rico signed an executive order for $8 million for the removal of debris and for the design of a new observatory to be built in its place. The governor stated reconstruction of the observatory is a "matter of public policy". The executive order also designated the area as a history site.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>As required by the <a href="/wiki/Consolidated_Appropriations_Act,_2021" title="Consolidated Appropriations Act, 2021">Consolidated Appropriations Act, 2021</a>, the NSF sent a report to Congress in March 2022 "on the causes and extent of the damage, the plan to remove debris in a safe and environmentally sound way, the preservation of the associated [Arecibo Observatory] facilities and surrounding areas, and the process for determining whether to establish comparable technology at the site, along with any associated cost estimates".<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  On March 25, 2022, a survey salvage committee formed by UCF and the NSF issued a final report, identifying materials from the site that may be salvaged for their "historic importance or scientific utility."<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>A team from the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Texas_at_Austin" title="University of Texas at Austin">University of Texas at Austin</a> was able to completely recover and back up the 3 <a href="/wiki/Petabyte" class="mw-redirect" title="Petabyte">petabytes</a> of data that the telescope had captured since opening in the 1960s by May 2021 before further harm could come to the storage equipment. The data was relocated to the school\'s servers at the <a href="/wiki/Texas_Advanced_Computing_Center" title="Texas Advanced Computing Center">Texas Advanced Computing Center</a> to be made available for continued research.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>An early plan developed by NSF scientists suggest one possible replacement called the Next Generation Arecibo Telescope, using 1000 closely-packed 9-meter (30&#160;ft) telescopes mounted on one or more flat plate(s) that would cover the 300-meter (980&#160;ft) width of the Arecibo sinkhole. While the telescopes themselves would be fixed, the plate(s) would be able to be rotated more than 45\xc2\xb0 off the horizontal in any direction. This would allow the new instrument to have 500 times the field of view of the original Arecibo Telescope, and be twice as sensitive with four times the radar power. It was expected this would cost roughly <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$450 million</span> to build.<sup id="cite_ref-ngat_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ngat-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This would enable better study of the <a href="/wiki/Galactic_Center#Supermassive_black_hole" title="Galactic Center">supermassive black hole at the center of the Milky Way</a> as a prime target.<sup id="cite_ref-science_collapse_16-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-science_collapse-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>NSF decided in October 2022 that the Arecibo site would not be used for a new telescope, instead converting the site to be a STEM educational center.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>The Arecibo Salvage Survey committee preserved some parts of the telescope, including parts of the zenith and azimuth tracks, a corner of the platform, the rotary joint, and the cable car.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Research_and_discoveries">Research and discoveries</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arecibo_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Research and discoveries"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<figure class="mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Arecibo_message.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Arecibo_message.svg/145px-Arecibo_message.svg.png" decoding="async" width="145" height="435" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Arecibo_message.svg/218px-Arecibo_message.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Arecibo_message.svg/290px-Arecibo_message.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="1536" /></a><figcaption>The <a href="/wiki/Arecibo_message" title="Arecibo message">Arecibo message</a> with added color to highlight the separate parts. The actual binary transmission carried no color information.</figcaption></figure>\n<p>Many scientific discoveries were made with the observatory. On April 7, 1964, soon after it began operating, <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Pettengill" title="Gordon Pettengill">Gordon Pettengill</a>\'s team used it to determine that the <a href="/wiki/Rotation" title="Rotation">rotation</a> period of <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a> was not 88 days, as formerly thought, but only 59 days.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 1968, the discovery of the periodicity of the <a href="/wiki/Crab_Pulsar" title="Crab Pulsar">Crab Pulsar</a> (33 milliseconds) by <a href="/wiki/Richard_V._E._Lovelace" title="Richard V. E. Lovelace">Richard V. E. Lovelace</a> and others provided the first solid evidence that <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star" title="Neutron star">neutron stars</a> exist.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 1974, <a href="/wiki/Russell_Alan_Hulse" title="Russell Alan Hulse">Hulse</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Hooton_Taylor,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Joseph Hooton Taylor, Jr.">Taylor</a> discovered the first binary pulsar <a href="/wiki/PSR_B1913%2B16" class="mw-redirect" title="PSR B1913+16">PSR B1913+16</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> an accomplishment for which they later received the Nobel Prize in Physics. In 1982, the first <a href="/wiki/Millisecond_pulsar" title="Millisecond pulsar">millisecond pulsar</a>, <a href="/wiki/PSR_B1937%2B21" title="PSR B1937+21">PSR B1937+21</a>, was discovered by <a href="/wiki/Donald_C._Backer" title="Donald C. Backer">Donald C. Backer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shrinivas_Kulkarni" title="Shrinivas Kulkarni">Shrinivas Kulkarni</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carl_Heiles" class="mw-redirect" title="Carl Heiles">Carl Heiles</a>, Michael Davis, and Miller Goss.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This object spins 642 times per second and, until the discovery of <a href="/wiki/PSR_J1748-2446ad" class="mw-redirect" title="PSR J1748-2446ad">PSR J1748-2446ad</a> in 2005, was identified as the fastest-spinning pulsar.\n</p><p>In 1980, Arecibo made the first radar observation of a comet when it successfully detected <a href="/wiki/Comet_Encke" title="Comet Encke">Comet Encke</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Campbell_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In August 1989, the observatory directly imaged an <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroid</a> for the first time in history: <a href="/wiki/4769_Castalia" title="4769 Castalia">4769 Castalia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The following year, Polish astronomer <a href="/wiki/Aleksander_Wolszczan" title="Aleksander Wolszczan">Aleksander Wolszczan</a> made the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Pulsar" title="Pulsar">pulsar</a> <a href="/wiki/PSR_B1257%2B12" title="PSR B1257+12">PSR B1257+12</a> (Lich), which later led him to discover its three orbiting planets.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These were the first <a href="/wiki/Extrasolar_planet" class="mw-redirect" title="Extrasolar planet">extrasolar planets</a> discovered. In 1994, John Harmon used the Arecibo Radio Telescope to map the distribution of ice in the polar regions of <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>93<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>In January 2008, detection of prebiotic molecules <a href="/wiki/Methylene_imine" title="Methylene imine">methanimine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_cyanide" title="Hydrogen cyanide">hydrogen cyanide</a> were reported from the observatory\'s radio spectroscopy measurements of the distant starburst galaxy <a href="/wiki/Arp_220" title="Arp 220">Arp 220</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ScienceDaily2008_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ScienceDaily2008-94"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>94<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>From January 2010 to February 2011, astronomers Matthew Route and <a href="/wiki/Aleksander_Wolszczan" title="Aleksander Wolszczan">Aleksander Wolszczan</a> detected bursts of radio emission from the T6.5 brown dwarf 2MASS J10475385+2124234. This was the first time that radio emission had been detected from a T dwarf, which has methane absorption lines in its atmosphere. It is also the coolest brown dwarf (at a temperature of ~900K) from which radio emission has been observed. The highly polarized and highly energetic radio bursts indicated that the object has a &gt;1.7&#160;<a href="/wiki/Gauss_(unit)" title="Gauss (unit)">kG</a>-strength magnetic field and magnetic activity similar to both the planet <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>95<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="The_Arecibo_message">The Arecibo message</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arecibo_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: The Arecibo message"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1236090951">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Arecibo_message" title="Arecibo message">Arecibo message</a></div>\n<p>In 1974, the <a href="/wiki/Arecibo_message" title="Arecibo message">Arecibo message</a>, an attempt to communicate with potential <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_life" title="Extraterrestrial life">extraterrestrial life</a>, was transmitted from the radio telescope toward the globular cluster <a href="/wiki/Messier_13" title="Messier 13">Messier 13</a>, about 25,000 light-years away.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>96<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The 1,679 <a href="/wiki/Bit" title="Bit">bit</a> pattern of 1s and 0s defined a 23 by 73 pixel <a href="/wiki/Bitmap" title="Bitmap">bitmap</a> image that included numbers, stick figures, chemical formulas and a crude image of the telescope.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>97<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="SETI_and_METI_projects">SETI and METI projects</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arecibo_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: SETI and METI projects"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/SETI" class="mw-redirect" title="SETI">SETI</a> and <a href="/wiki/Active_SETI" title="Active SETI">Active SETI</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Search_for_extraterrestrial_intelligence" title="Search for extraterrestrial intelligence">Search for extraterrestrial intelligence</a> (SETI)<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>98<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> is the search for extraterrestrial life or advanced technologies. SETI aims to answer the question "Are we alone in the Universe?" by scanning the skies for transmissions from intelligent civilizations elsewhere in our galaxy.\n</p><p>In comparison, METI (messaging to extraterrestrial intelligence) refers to the <a href="/wiki/Active_SETI" title="Active SETI">active search</a> by transmitting messages.\n</p><p>Arecibo was the source of data for the <a href="/wiki/SETI@home" title="SETI@home">SETI@home</a> and <a href="/wiki/Astropulse" title="Astropulse">Astropulse</a> <a href="/wiki/Distributed_computing" title="Distributed computing">distributed computing</a> projects put forward by the Space Sciences Laboratory at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Berkeley" title="University of California, Berkeley">University of California, Berkeley</a>, and was used for the <a href="/wiki/SETI_Institute" title="SETI Institute">SETI Institute</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Project_Phoenix_(SETI)" title="Project Phoenix (SETI)">Project Phoenix</a> observations.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>99<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Einstein@Home" title="Einstein@Home">Einstein@Home</a> distributed computing project has found more than 20 <a href="/wiki/Pulsar" title="Pulsar">pulsars</a> in Arecibo data.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>100<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Other_uses">Other uses</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arecibo_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Other uses"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<p>Terrestrial aeronomy experiments at Arecibo included the <a href="/wiki/NASA_Coqui" class="mw-redirect" title="NASA Coqui">Coqui 2</a> experiment, supported by <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>. The telescope also originally had <a href="/wiki/Military_intelligence" title="Military intelligence">military intelligence</a> uses, including locating <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet</a> <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">radar</a> installations by detecting their signals <a href="/wiki/EME_(communications)" class="mw-redirect" title="EME (communications)">bouncing</a> off the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>101<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>Limited amateur radio operations were carried out, using <i>moon bounce</i> or <a href="/wiki/Earth%E2%80%93Moon%E2%80%93Earth_communication" title="Earth\xe2\x80\x93Moon\xe2\x80\x93Earth communication">Earth\xe2\x80\x93Moon\xe2\x80\x93Earth communication</a>, in which radio signals aimed at the Moon are reflected back to Earth. The first of these operations was on June 13\xe2\x80\x9314, 1964, using the call sign KP4BPZ. A dozen or so two-way contacts were made on 144 and 432&#160;MHz. On July 3 and 24, 1965, KP4BPZ was again activated on 432&#160;MHz, making approximately 30 contacts on 432&#160;MHz during the limited time slots available. For these tests, a very wide-band instrumentation recorder captured a large segment of the receiving bandwidth, enabling later verification of other amateur station call signs. These were not two-way contacts. From April 16\xe2\x80\x9318, 2010, the Arecibo Amateur Radio Club KP4AO again conducted moon-bounce activity using the antenna.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>102<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> On November 10, 2013, the KP4AO Arecibo Amateur Radio Club conducted a Fifty-Year Commemoration Activation, lasting seven hours on 14.250&#160;MHz SSB, without using the main dish antenna.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Cultural_significance">Cultural significance</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arecibo_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Cultural significance"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<p>Due to its unique shape and concept, the telescope had been featured in many contemporary works. It serves as one of the central locations in <a href="/wiki/The_Sparrow_(novel)" title="The Sparrow (novel)"><i>The Sparrow</i></a>, a science fiction novel written by <a href="/wiki/Mary_Doria_Russell" title="Mary Doria Russell">Mary Doria Russell</a>. It was used as a filming location in the films <i><a href="/wiki/GoldenEye" title="GoldenEye">GoldenEye</a></i> (1995), <i><a href="/wiki/Species_(film)" title="Species (film)">Species</a></i> (1995), and <i><a href="/wiki/Contact_(1997_American_film)" title="Contact (1997 American film)">Contact</a></i> (1997) (based on <a href="/wiki/Carl_Sagan" title="Carl Sagan">Carl Sagan</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Contact_(novel)" title="Contact (novel)">novel of the same name</a>, which also featured the observatory), <i><a href="/wiki/The_Losers_(2010_film)" title="The Losers (2010 film)">The Losers</a></i> (2010),<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-drake_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-drake-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and in <i><a href="/wiki/The_X-Files" title="The X-Files">The X-Files</a></i> television episode "<a href="/wiki/Little_Green_Men_(The_X-Files)" title="Little Green Men (The X-Files)">Little Green Men</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>105<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> One map in the 2013 video game <i><a href="/wiki/Battlefield_4" title="Battlefield 4">Battlefield 4</a></i>, while set in China, is based on the distinctive layout of the Arecibo Telescope.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>106<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 2014, a video art installation piece titled <i>The Great Silence</i> by artists <a href="/wiki/Allora_%26_Calzadilla" title="Allora &amp; Calzadilla">Jennifer Allora and Guillermo Calzadilla</a> in collaboration with science fiction writer <a href="/wiki/Ted_Chiang" title="Ted Chiang">Ted Chiang</a> featured the radio telescope at Arecibo Observatory to represent the search for extraterrestrial life. The next year, Chiang published a novelette also called <a href="/wiki/The_Great_Silence_(story)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Great Silence (story)">The Great Silence</a>. The juxtaposed text was later published as a short story with the same title in a special issue of the art journal <i><a href="/wiki/E-flux_publications" title="E-flux publications">e-flux</a></i> in 2015 and was included in the author\'s short story collection <i><a href="/wiki/Exhalation:_Stories" title="Exhalation: Stories">Exhalation: Stories</a></i> in 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>107<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>The asteroid <a href="/wiki/4337_Arecibo" title="4337 Arecibo">4337 Arecibo</a> is named after the observatory by <a href="/wiki/Steven_J._Ostro" title="Steven J. Ostro">Steven J. Ostro</a>, in recognition of the observatory\'s contributions to the characterization of Solar System bodies.<sup id="cite_ref-MPC-object_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MPC-object-108"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>108<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="See_also">See also"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/List_of_radio_telescopes" title="List of radio telescopes">List of radio telescopes</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Orgov_Radio-Optical_Telescope" title="Orgov Radio-Optical Telescope">Orgov Radio-Optical Telescope</a></li></ul>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2
